People v. Battle


Crim. A. No. 13180


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


50 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 1; 123 Cal. Rptr. 636; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1348



June 27, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Newhall Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. CRM 4578, Adrian W. Adams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prosecution sought review of the judgment of the Municipal Court for the Newhall Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed charges brought against respondent criminal defendant under Cal. Penal Code § 192(3)(b) for a double jeopardy violation.


OVERVIEW: Respondent criminal defendant pled nolo contendere to a violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 26453 which was based upon a vehicular accident which killed three people. Respondent was later charged with three misdemeanor violations of Cal. Penal Code § 192(3)(b). The charges were dismissed by the trial court for a violation of double jeopardy. The court reversed, holding that a trial court could not bar prosecution of a misdemeanor by reason of an earlier plea to an infraction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order dismissing the charges against respondent criminal defendant on double jeopardy grounds. The court held that a plea to an infraction or petty offense would not bar later prosecution for a misdemeanor for the same offense.

 

1939 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kruger


Crim. A. No. 12949


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


48 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 15; 121 Cal. Rptr. 581; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1179



May 1, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 862267, Glenn A. Wymore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant fisherman appealed a decision of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of unlawful and willful possession of yellow fin tuna in excess of 15 percent by round weight of his catch under Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 108.


OVERVIEW: A complaint was filed against defendant fisherman charging him with violation of the Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 108, adopted pursuant to 50 C.F.R. § 280.7(b)(2). The complaint alleged that defendant was in charge of a fishing vessel after the closing of the yellow fin tuna season, entered the regulated fishing area, and willfully and unlawfully possessed on board an amount of yellow fin tuna in excess of 15 percent by round weight of his total catch. The trial court convicted defendant, placed him on probation, and ordered that his fish be forfeited. On appeal the court reversed defendant's conviction. The court concluded that the state regulation under which defendant was charged was an infringement of a preempted area of law. The court held that while existing statutes may be incorporated by reference, prospective incorporation had never been approved by a California court. Defendant may not have gained knowledge of the federal law because the regulation may have been adopted after he had checked the federal register. Such notice did not afford a person of ordinary intelligence a reasonable opportunity to know what was prohibited so that he may act accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant fisherman's conviction under a state regulation for unlawful possession of yellow fin tuna in excess of 15 percent by round weight of his catch. The state regulation infringed on a preempted area of law covered by a federal regulation, and notice of the federal regulation did not afford defendant a reasonable opportunity to know what conduct was prohibited.

 

1940 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Allheim


Crim. A. Nos. 13038, 13044


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


48 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 1; 121 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1177



March 31, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Pomona Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M 68260, James H. Piatt, Judge.

Municipal Court for the Glendale Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M 50003, Richard K. Grace, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal in the Roy Douglas Phillips matter, Crim. A No. 13044, is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged orders of the Municipal Court for the Pomona Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their motions to find prior convictions declared unconstitutional in cases charging one defendant with driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor and causing bodily injury and convicting the other defendant with driving under the influence of liquor and drugs.


OVERVIEW: One defendant was charged with driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor and causing bodily injury and appealed before trial while another defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of liquor and drugs but appealed before sentencing. Both defendants appealed the denial of their motions to declare a prior conviction unconstitutional. On appeal, the court dismissed both appeals and held that a motion to vacate a judgment of conviction had to be made in the rendering court and in the same case, not in the trial court in a different case. The court also found that a motion to strike a prior could not lie unless the prior was alleged in the accusatory pleading. The second defendant's verdict was not appealable as there had been no sentence nor order granting probation and there was no final judgment of conviction.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed both appeals by defendants of the denial of their motions to have prior convictions declared unconstitutional. The court held that one verdict was not appealable as there was no final judgment of conviction and that a motion to vacate judgment had to be made in the same case and in the rendering court.

 

1941 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Oppenheimer


Crim. A. No. 12647


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


42 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 4; 116 Cal. Rptr. 795; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1291



August 14, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 20 99804, Maurice J. Hindin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contested a judgment from the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, (California), arguing that he had a constitutional right to a jury trial when he was charged with the commission of an infraction, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 21453, for failing to stop at a traffic signal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant received a citation alleging that he was in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 21453 when he failed to stop for a traffic signal. He was convicted and appealed. Appellant contended that the issue existed as to whether one charged with, and convicted of, the commission of an infraction in violation of this section was unconstitutionally deprived of the right to a jury trial. The appellate court held that such a person was not entitled to a jury trial, and affirmed the judgment. It stated that offenses of the same class as those now classified as infractions by the California Vehicle Code were historically triable by courts sitting without juries. Tested by any measure, the infraction of which appellant was convicted had to be classified as petty. Therefore, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed appellant's conviction for the traffic infraction of failing to stop at a traffic signal, and affirmed the lower court's determination that appellant had no constitutional right to trial by jury in this case. The court found no historical right to a jury trial for petty offenses such as this infraction.

People v. M. V. Nurseries, Inc.

Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


40 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 1; 115 Cal. Rptr. 326; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 937

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M135088, William R. Hollingsworth, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed its conviction from the Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), for deliveries of nursery items within Manhattan Beach without payment of a license tax imposed by the city on each vehicle under Manhattan Beach Cal. Mun. Code § 6-2.30(v).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of deliveries of nursery items within Manhattan Beach without payment of a license tax imposed by the city on each vehicle under Manhattan Beach Cal. Mun. The court reversed the conviction because the ordinance was arbitrary, discriminatory and unconstitutional. OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction from the lower court for deliveries of nursery items within Manhattan Beach without payment of a license tax imposed by the city on each vehicle was reversed because the statute at issue was unconstitutional.

People v. Man

1944 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Olague


Crim. A. No. 11667


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


31 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 5; 106 Cal. Rptr. 612; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1129



February 21, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the East Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M 126570, Harold M. Stanley, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order of the Municipal Court for the East Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent father's petition for demurrer on the basis that Cal. Penal Code § 270 was unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state sought review of the trial court's order which granted respondent father's petition for demurrer on the basis that Cal. Penal Code § 270 was unconstitutional on it face and created an invidious discrimination between fathers and mothers, thereby denying fathers equal protection of the law. The court reversed the judgment. The court held that the limited and peculiar nature of the interest asserted by respondent was not of the fundamental kind of right which invoked an attitude of critical analysis, subjecting the classification to strict scrutiny and to proof of a compelling state interest. Respondent's desire to resist and defeat his duty to support his child unless and until appellant proceeded against the mother did not invoke subjecting classification to strict scrutiny. The court concluded that it was the legal duty of both parents to support their children, but that in the existing state of society, fathers generally were better situated to meet the obligation. The court opined that the fact that there could be exceptions such as indigent or dissolute fathers, or wealthy and independent mothers, did not vitiate the reasonableness of § 270.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granting respondent father's demurrer. The court held that respondent's desire to resist and defeat his duty to support his child unless and until appellant state proceeded against the mother was not of the fundamental kind of right that invoked an attitude of critical analysis, subjecting the classification to strict scrutiny.

 

1945 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Turner v. Evers


Civ. A. No. 226117


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Sacramento


31 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 11; 107 Cal. Rptr. 390; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1130



January 3, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Sacramento Judicial District of Sacramento County, No. 84180, Lawrence W. Marvin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of July 18, 1972, quashing service of summons and, in effect, quashing the attachment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought reversal of the order of the Municipal Court for the Sacramento Judicial District of Sacramento County (California) that quashed, for lack of jurisdiction, the attachment of an insurer's obligation to defend and indemnify.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, residents of California, had filed an action against respondent, a resident of Washington, seeking monetary damages for respondent's failure to properly service appellant's automobile. Arguing that it constituted property, appellants attached respondent's insurance company's contractual obligation to defend and indemnify respondent in case of a civil suit. Respondent moved to quash, challenging both personal and quasi in rem jurisdiction. The motion was granted and appellant sought review. The court reversed the lower court's order. It held that a contractual obligation to indemnify constituted a property right, one that furnished a nexus with the forum state, was present in the state, and was created upon filing of the action. It was thus subject to attachment. Additionally, the debt was attachable because the obligation to defend was certain; only the amount of damages was uncertain. Finally, respondent's argument claiming state law prohibited direct actions against insurers was a misreading of the law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order quashing an attachment of an insurer's obligation to defend and indemnify because the insurer's contractual duty was a certain debt and created a property right that was present in the state, and therefore subject to attachment.

People v. Deem

People v. Adler


Crim. A. No. 35718


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Sacramento County


25 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 24; 101 Cal. Rptr. 726; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1108



March 21, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Sacramento Judicial District of Sacramento County, Robert N. Zarick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged their convictions in the Municipal Court for the Sacramento Judicial District of Sacramento County (California) under Cal. Penal Code § 311.2 for selling and possessing obscene films and an obscene book.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the convictions of appellants, who were found to have sold and possessed obscene films and a book under Cal. Penal Code § 311.2. Applying the Roth test, it held that the book was obscene, finding that it appealed to a prurient interest in sex, was patently offensive by contemporary community standards, and was without redeeming social value. The court found that the jury instructions accurately reflected the standard used to define obscenity and correctly defined the state of California as the area to be considered in determining community standards, writing that even if there had been error it would have been harmless beyond a reasonable doubt because the materials were unquestionably obscene. It rejected appellants' argument that the people had failed to prove that they had knowledge of the book's contents, noting that appellants were adult booksellers who worked on site and that their knowledge could be established through this circumstantial evidence. Finally, it found that the procedures used in issuing and executing the search warrant for the materials were more than adequate to avoid suppression of constitutionally protected publications.


OUTCOME: Finding that the book and films appellants were convicted of selling and possessing were unquestionably obscene and that the jury instructions were without error, the court upheld appellants' convictions. It also found that appellants' knowledge of the book's contents had been proved by circumstantial evidence and that the films were seized pursuant to a valid search warrant.

 

1948 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cruz


Crim. A. Nos. 10643, 10644, 10645, 10646


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


25 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 1; 101 Cal. Rptr. 711; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1107



March 2, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, Nos. 352416 and 352417, David J. Aisenson and Julius M. Title, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants were convicted of violation of Cal. Penal Code § 302, disruption of a religious service, in the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California). Appellants sought review based on the trial court's failure to grant their motion for continuance and for substitution of counsel; the misconduct of the jury; and the unconstitutionality of § 302.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code § 302 and other violations. On appeal, defendants urged that the trial court erred in refusing to grant their motion for substitution of counsel, that the jury committed misconduct, and that § 302 was unconstitutionally vague. The court affirmed the convictions. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to allow a substitution of counsel, given that defendants had ample time due to continuances to procure other counsel, but did not, and did not renew their objection at trial. The court held that conversations overheard by jurors did not constitute discussions of issues in the case; nor was it improper for the jury forewoman to draw up a diagram and list of issues for the jurors to consider. The court held that § 302 was not unconstitutionally vague because defendants were on notice that their conduct in banging on doors and windows, screaming and running through the church, and assaulting churchgoers, would constitute "rude behavior" and "unnecessary noise" within the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions. The trial court's failure to grant a continuance or substitution of counsel was not an abuse of discretion in the circumstances. Nor did the trial court abuse his discretion in finding an absence of prejudicial misconduct by the jury in the circumstances. The statute under which defendants were convicted was not unconstitutionally vague, nor did the trial judge's charge render it an ex post facto law.

 

1949 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Foretich


Crim. A. Nos. 9599, 9600, 9601, 9602, 9603, 9604


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


14 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 6; 92 Cal. Rptr. 481; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1211



December 31, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District of Los Angeles County, George R. Perkovich, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal in each case is reversed, and each case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings not inconsistent herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, the state challenged judgments from the Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) dismissing charges against defendant fisherman that alleged that defendants violated Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8603. The trial court found that the illegal activity occurred three miles offshore and thus not within the state's boundaries and that the state had no authority to regulate the activity.


OVERVIEW: In consolidated appeals, the state appealed judgments that dismissed charges against defendant fisherman that alleged that defendants used an illegal purse seine net, in violation of Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8603, while fishing within California Fish and Game District 19a, as defined in Cal. Fish & Game Code § 11028. The court reversed the judgments. The reviewing court rejected defendants' contentions, as accepted by the trial court, that defendants' illegal activity, which occurred three miles offshore, was not within the state's boundaries and that the state had no authority to regulate the activity. The court ruled that prevailing case law restricted only the state's power to convey real property; the cases did not determine the political boundaries of the state. Thus, the court ruled that Santa Monica Bay was included within the boundaries of the state, the trial court erred in holding that defendant's conduct occurred outside the state's boundaries.


OUTCOME: The court reversed judgments that dismissed charges against defendant fisherman that alleged that they used an illegal fishing net. Under the applicable statutes and prior case law, the illegal activity occurred within the state's boundaries, though three miles offshore. Regardless, as citizens of the state, defendants were subject to prosecution by the state for violating state law.

 

1950 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lindenbaum


Crim. A. No. 9444


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


11 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 1; 90 Cal. Rptr. 340; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1812



September 4, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Pomona Judicial District of Los Angeles County, Norman L. Vroman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of dismissal are reversed with instructions to the trial court to overrule the demurrers and allow defendants to plead to the complaints.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: People appealed from orders of Municipal Court for Pomona Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained respondents' demurrers to criminal complaints charging them with violating City of Pomona, Cal., Ordinance 1673, § 14.4-5, by nude performances in bars and by procuring such performers, on ground ordinance was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondents were charged with violating a city ordinance, which prohibited nude dancing in city bars. The municipal court sustained the demurrers to the criminal complaints on the ground the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court reversed. Cal. Penal Code §§ 318.5 and 318.6 permitted the city to adopt this type of ordinance free from the restraint of preemption. Further, the ordinance was constitutional. Where speech and nonspeech elements were combined in the same course of conduct, a sufficiently important governmental interest in regulating the nonspeech aspect would justify incidental limitations on First Amendment freedoms. The city council found in the ordinance's preamble that nude performances in bars involved police problems that substantially increased the police department's workload and provided greater opportunity for lewd conduct thereby adversely affecting the general welfare. Thus, there was a substantial governmental interest served in regulating businesses that caused police problems. This governmental interest was unrelated to the suppression of free speech; and the incidental restriction was no greater than was essential to the furtherance of that interest.


OUTCOME: Court reversed judgments of dismissal, holding California Penal Code permitted city to adopt this ordinance free from preemption restraint. Ordinance was constitutional because there was substantial governmental interest to be served in regulating businesses that caused police problems and restrictions were no greater than was essential to further that interest.

 

1951 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Horton


Crim. A. Nos. 8632, 8633, 8634, 8635


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


9 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 1; 87 Cal. Rptr. 818; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2018



May 21, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Inglewood Judicial District of Los Angeles County, Stephen R. Stothers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Since the above premises are incorrect, and since our independent examination of the whole record does not disclose any violation of any constitutional right of any defendant, and since the statute was clearly violated, we must and do affirm the orders granting probation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed orders of the Municipal Court for the Inglewood Judicial District of Los Angeles County, California, that granted probation for violation of Cal. Penal Code § 602.9. Defendants contended that state laws relating to pedestrian and vehicular traffic were subordinate to the U.S. Const. amend. I rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed orders of the trial court that granted probation for violation of Cal. Penal Code § 602.9. Defendants were arrested after participating in a demonstration at the entrance to a college campus. The court affirmed the orders on the basis that the state established the elements of the crime. The court found that defendants were on school property or adjacent property without any lawful business. The court stated that the assertion of U.S. Const. amend. I rights did not permit defendants to flout traffic laws and that picketing in a manner that interfered with traffic was clearly not lawful business as defined by § 602.9. The court stated that continuation of the conduct after defendants were requested to leave exhibited the general intent required by the statute. Further, the court found that defendants disrupted school activities by obstructing the entrance to the school and that after being asked to leave, defendants continued to pass out leaflets and talk to drivers who were forced to slow down and stop because of the group activity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders that granted probation because independent examination of the whole record revealed no violation of any constitutional right of the defendants and revealed the elements of the offense were clearly established.

 

1952 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hairston


Crim. A. No. 8952


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


8 Cal. App. 3d Supp. 19; 87 Cal. Rptr. 470; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2124



May 5, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, Alan G. Campbell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the order of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him under Cal. Penal Code § 409 for unlawful assembly during a period of college campus unrest.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, during a period of campus unrest, was arrested for remaining at a meeting after issuance of an administrative order prohibiting all meetings except classes, and charged with unlawful assembly under Cal. Penal Code § 409. Defendant refused to present a defense, was convicted, and refused probation. Defendant appealed. The court found that state showed that the order banning assemblies on campus was properly authorized and that there was sufficient evidence of the threat of violence prior to the meeting to justify its issuance. The temporary order was not overbroad given the emergency that precipitated the ruling, and it clearly furthered school administration. The court concluded that school officials had to protect the constitutional rights of all, even a majority, in danger of violence. Furthermore, there was no duty to consult with student groups before issuing the order. Accordingly, the conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for unlawful assembly. The court found that state presented substantial evidence to support the finding that defendant's college's president validly issued the administrative order prohibiting student assembly in response to campus violence and that defendant could be convicted of unlawful assembly under the requisite criminal statute.

People v. Maxwell


Crim. A. No. 50


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Ventura


275 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 1026; 80 Cal. Rptr. 86; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2013



June 25, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from judgments of the Municipal Court of the Ventura Judicial District of Ventura County. Albert T. Blanford, Judge.

Prosecutions for possessing, on a boat, fish in such condition that the species cannot be determined.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgments of dismissal reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff the People sought review of judgments of the Municipal Court of the Ventura Judicial District of Ventura County (California), which dismissed the prosecution against defendants for possessing, on a boat, fish in such condition that the species could not be determined in violation of Cal. Fish & Game Code § 5509.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted defendants' motions to suppress evidence of the contents of the sacks held by the passengers upon departing the fishing vessels upon which defendants were deckhands. The fish and game wardens had no cause to believe that either passenger had committed any crime and had no reason to believe that the sacks contained fish which were in such condition that the species could not be determined. The determinative question was whether a warden could have searched a sack without a warrant. Defendants contended that any right of inspection was conditioned upon a demand being first made by a warden that fish be exhibited and that no such demand was made. The court did not read Cal. Fish & Game Code § 2012 as qualifying all of the other provisions of the code relating to inspection. Defendants next contended that if the search was purportedly authorized by statute, it nevertheless was an unconstitutional search and seizure. The court found that the search made and the subsequent seizure of fish, each pursuant to statute, were not unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments.

 

1954 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cowgill


Crim. A. No. 8379.


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


274 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 923; 78 Cal. Rptr. 853; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2132



May 27, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court of the South Bay Judicial District of Los Angeles County granting probation in a prosecution for publicly mutilating, defacing, defiling or trampling upon a flag of the United States. Auten F. Bush, Judge. Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Municipal Court of the South Bay Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) granting probation following his conviction for publicly mutilating, defacing, defiling, or trampling upon a flag of the United States, a violation of Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 614(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant cut a United States flag and sewed it into a vest that he wore in public. In appealing his probation order defendant alleged that § 614(d) was unconstitutionally overbroad and could not be enforced. The court on appeal affirmed the probation order. Under principles of statutory construction the statute was presumed to be constitutional and was to be given a constitutional interpretation if it was susceptible to two interpretations. In construing the statute the court held that the statute could not be violated by the use of words only. The general connotation of the statute was that it was meant to apply acts of flag desecration and not words. Therefore, use of the word "defile" in the statute did not render the statute unconstitutional. In addition, there was a legitimate state interest in protecting the flag, preventing violence, and maintaining the public peace that justified incidental limitations of the protection afforded symbolic speech by the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting probation.

 

1955 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hardin


Crim. No. 11673


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


256 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 954; 64 Cal. Rptr. 307; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1941



November 16, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County dismissing a criminal complaint against defendants. Charles M. Snell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People appealed from an order of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County (California), which dismissed the charges against defendants for the misdemeanor of being present in a place where narcotics were being unlawfully used, on the grounds that defendants were not brought to trial within the time limit set forth in Cal. Penal Code § 1382.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were initially arraigned before a magistrate on a felony and misdemeanor count. The magistrate found the evidence of the felony insufficient, and dismissed the charges. He ordered defendants to appear before a judge in the superior court, which remanded them on the misdemeanor count to the municipal court. When their trial date was set in the municipal court nearly two months after their initial appearance before the magistrate, they moved to dismiss the misdemeanor count because they were not brought to trial within the time limits of § 1382. On appeal, the court held that the municipal court erred in granting defendants' motion to dismiss. The court ruled that because defendants initially appeared before a magistrate, they were not arraigned on any criminal complaint in an "inferior court." Thus, the court held, dismissal under § 1382 was not available until they appeared before the municipal court, which was less than 30 days from the date set for trial. The court held that § 1382 dealt with trials, therefore, the municipal court erred in holding that § 1382 became operative upon defendants' arraignment before the magistrate, which invoked no trial court jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the municipal court's order granting defendants' motion to dismiss. The court remanded the case to the municipal court with directions to proceed with the trial on the misdemeanor count.

People v. Kukkanen

Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles

January 4, 1967

Prosecutions for failing to have a "live entertainment license" in a public place selling food or beverages involving the presence of a "topless waitress."



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgments of dismissal reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with operating a bar with topless waitresses without a permit in violation of Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 103.102. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered a judgment dismissing the charges on the grounds that § 103.102 was unconstitutional as pre-empted by state law. The State appealed in four cases, which the court consolidated on appeal.


OVERVIEW: In each case, defendants operated a business where food or beverages were sold and served by topless waitresses. Defendants operated their businesses without obtaining a live entertainment license from the Los Angeles Police Commissioner as required by Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 103.102. The trial court granted defendants' motions to dismiss the charges on the grounds that § 103.102 was unconstitutional as pre-empted by Cal. Penal Code § 650 1/2, which preempted all municipal regulation of criminal sexual activity and public indecency. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that Los Angeles, Cal., Code § 103.102 was not preempted because it regulated live entertainment and did not reach criminal sexual activity and public indecency.

1957 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Green


Crim. Nos. A6153, A6154, A6155, A6156, A6157, A6158


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


234 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 871; 44 Cal. Rptr. 438; 1965 Cal. App. LEXIS 1076



April 29, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from judgments of the Municipal Court of the Santa Monica Judicial District of Los Angeles County. W. Blair Gibbens, Judge.

Prosecutions for disturbing the peace.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants in six cases appealed judgments from the Municipal Court of the Santa Monica Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) convicting them of disturbing the peace, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 415.


OVERVIEW: Defendants and others had been picketing outside a bank but then entered the bank, blocked the entrance to the bank, and disrupted the business of the bank and its customers. The court on appeal affirmed defendants' convictions. The court held that the evidence established beyond a reasonable doubt that defendants interfered with the normal business of the bank and were guilty of disturbing the peace. The conduct of defendants only had to disturb the public peace. It was not necessary that defendants' acts were violent in order to constitute a breach of the peace under § 415. The court noted that cases cited by defendants were not on point, particularly cases where demonstrations or picketing occurred on a street as opposed to a private business establishment. The court also rejected a claim that § 415 was unconstitutional, holding that it provided objective notice of the conduct prohibited and was therefore neither vague nor indefinite.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions.

 

1958 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Poe


Crim. No. 7617


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


236 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 928; 47 Cal. Rptr. 670; 1965 Cal. App. LEXIS 894; 1 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9714



April 23, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from judgments of the Municipal Court of the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County. Roy G. Fitzgerald, Judge.

Prosecutions for trespass and for willful disobedience of a court order.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgments of convictions reversed as to one count and affirmed as to all other counts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of their convictions for trespass in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 602(j), 602(l), and 166.4 from the Municipal Court of the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of trespass on property of a bank. The trespass was charged in two counts: entering land with intention to interfere with or obstruct a business ( Cal. Penal Code § 602(j)) and entering and occupying property without owner's consent ( Cal. Penal Code § 602(l)). A third count charged willful disobedience of a superior court order ( Cal. Penal Code § 166.4), which restrained defendants from trespassing on or about the property of the bank. On appeal, the court conclude that § 602(l) could not be applied to make a mere occupancy without consent a criminal act, where the preceding entry was not also "without consent." These convictions were reversed. There was ample evidence to justify the verdict of guilty on the § 602(j) charge. The willful disobedience convictions were affirmed because trial judge did not err in instructing the jury that personal service of the order upon defendants and knowledge of defendants that they had been served with a temporary restraining order were prerequisites for finding defendants guilty of a willful violation of such order.


OUTCOME: The judgments as to entering and occupying property without the owner's consent were reversed. In all other respects the judgments were affirmed.

People v. Bongiorni

Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


205 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 856; 23 Cal. Rptr. 565; 1962 Cal. App. LEXIS 2208



June 22, 1962

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with a violation of Cal. Elec. Code § 12049(a)-(b). The Municipal Court for the Oceanside Judicial District of San Diego County (California) granted defendant's motion to dismiss on the ground that the statute was void on its face. After the trial court entered a judgment that dismissed the action, the People sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused of violating § 12049(a)-(b) by causing to be distributed a circular letter opposing a proposed sewer bond issue for a city to be voted upon at an election, which circular letter did not bear the name or address of the person or persons responsible for its issuance and distribution. The trial court dismissed the action, finding that § 12049(a)-(b) was facially invalid. The People appealed. The court affirmed, holding that § 12049(a)-(b) was void on its face because it invaded defendant's freedom of speech in violation of U.S. Const. amends. I, XIV. The court relied on a United States Supreme Court opinion that held a similar ordinance unconstitutional. Contrary to the People's argument, the court was unable to find sufficient distinction between the distribution of handbills generally and the distribution of handbills concerning elections to justify departure from the rule laid down by the United States Supreme Court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

1961 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cantor


Crim. A. No. 4792


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


198 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 843; 18 Cal. Rptr. 363; 1961 Cal. App. LEXIS 2602



December 19, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District. Kenneth L. Holaday, Judge.

Prosecution for practicing medicine without a license.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hypnotist challenged a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California) which convicted him of practicing medicine without a license in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2141.


OVERVIEW: The hypnotist was convicted of practicing medicine without a license on two different dates in violation of § 2141. He sought review of his conviction. The court affirmed, holding that the judgment was supported by evidence that he practiced hypnosis and diagnosed and treated two persons for ailments, diseases, and disorders. There was also evidence that the hypnotist advertised his services. The fact that he did not claim to be a physician was not dispositive because he publicly professed to be able to diagnose and treat human ailments. The court also found that appellant was not entrapped by a state board medical examiner during her questioning of him because appellant denied the commission of the crime. Because he advertised and practiced hypnotism in the line of his regular business, he was caught, not entrapped. In addition, the court found that § 2141 was not unconstitutional because it was not so vague or uncertain as to make it impossible for a defendant properly to defend himself.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the hypnotist's conviction for practicing medicine without a license.

 

1962 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hellen v. Stephenson


Civ. A. No. 257393


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


197 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 863; 18 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1961 Cal. App. LEXIS 1420



December 1, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District. Joseph A. Kilgarif, Judge.

Action to foreclose a mechanic's lien.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for defendant after demurrer to complaint was sustained without leave to amend, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trustee sought review of the judgment of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District (California) in favor of defendant property owners, in the trustee's action to foreclose a mechanic's lien.


OVERVIEW: The trustee, as the trustee in bankruptcy for the estate of the debtor corporation, sought to foreclose a mechanic's lien upon the property owners' property. The trial court sustained the demurrer. It was undisputed that the trustee failed to comply with the provisions of Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1193, which required written notice of an intent to file a mechanic's lien 15 days prior to filing the claim of lien. As such, the sole question on appeal involved the validity of § 1193. Relying on a prior decision, the court ruled that § 1193 was not valid. Specifically, the court relied upon the fact that the California Constitution placed mechanics, materialmen, artisans, and laborers in the same class, and that this equality thus could not have been impaired or destroyed by the California Legislature. The court rejected the argument that the prior decision dealt with substantive matters, while § 1193 was procedural in nature. The court explained that even though a procedural classification might have been proper, it still had to have been reasonable and based on some legitimate difference. There was no sound basis for the distinction between the different types of laborers.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, and the court remanded the matter to the trial court with directions to overrule the demurrer and permit the filing of an answer.

People v. Balmer

Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles

Prosecution for owning and operating a nursing and convalescent home in violation of certain state regulations.

1964 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stipp


Crim. A. No. 4529


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


190 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 883; 12 Cal. Rptr. 476; 1961 Cal. App. LEXIS 2385



March 30, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District and from an order denying a new trial. Joseph A. Wapner, Judge.

Prosecution of driver of vehicle for failure to stop and supply necessary information after striking an "unattended" motor vehicle.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed; appeal from order dismissed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction by the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California) for violating Cal. Veh. Code § 20007 when he failed to stop and supply the necessary information after striking an "unattended" vehicle. The trial court also denied defendant's motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's vehicle struck the complainant's vehicle in a parking lot adjacent to a cafe. The complainant was in the cafe but saw the accident. He immediately went to the scene and after an exchange of words, defendant left without supplying the information required by § 20007. On appeal, defendant argued that § 20007 was unconstitutional because of the uncertainty in the meaning of the word "unattended." Defendant also contended that the complainant's vehicle was attended and that he was therefore, charged with a violation of the wrong statute. The court found that the meaning of "unattended" in § 20007 was sufficiently certain and that the statute was constitutional. The court held, however, that to constitute an attended vehicle it was not necessary that the attending person be in the car when it was struck. It was only necessary that he be on or appear on the scene in time for defendant to give him the information required by Cal. Veh. Code § 20005. This interpretation of "unattended" excluded the circumstances here, where the owner of the vehicle was at the scene and could speak for himself even though he was not in the vehicle at the time of the collision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for failing to stop and supply the necessary information after striking an "unattended" vehicle.

 

1965 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Leverson


Crim. A. No. 4444


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


185 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 863; 8 Cal. Rptr. 734; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1590



October 14, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District. Kenneth L. Holaday, Judge.

Prosecution for violation of Bus. & Prof. Code, § 600, prohibiting the distribution of advertising or other printed matter concerning venereal diseases, lost manhood, etc.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which convicted him of violating Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 600. Section 600 prohibited the distribution of advertising or other printed matter that concerned venereal diseases, lost manhood, etc.


OVERVIEW: Defendant attacked § 600 on the ground that it violated the First Amendment in that it infringed on the freedom of the press. The court reversed. The court held that the dissemination of printed material fell within the safeguard of the First Amendment. Further, the court noted that § 600 made no distinction between the dissemination of advertising that was truthful and that called attention to medicines or treatments for the listed diseases or conditions that were approved by recognized medical authorities as beneficial and those that were dangerous and the advertising of which was false and fraudulent. Moreover, the court noted that while not every publication was protected by the First Amendment, publication of beneficial information about drugs or treatment was. In addition, the court held that § 600 was unconstitutional because it was tantamount to censorship. According to the court, § 600 attempted to give the Department of Health absolute power to say who could distribute the publications. Finally, the court held that § 600 was void because it gave to the Executive Branch of the Government absolute and arbitrary power to forbid anyone to distribute the advertising.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the action with directions to dismiss.

 

1966 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Keith


Crim. A. No. 4418


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


184 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 884; 7 Cal. Rptr. 613; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1950



September 8, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Beverly Hills Judicial District and from an order denying a new trial. Henry H. Draeger, Judge.

Prosecution for driving a vehicle while under the combined influence of intoxicating liquor and a drug.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Beverly Hills Judicial District (California), which convicted him of driving a vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor and a drug and from an order denying him a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction arose from an accidental overdose of insulin combined with taking two drinks containing vodka. Defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient, that an instruction was erroneous, and that the use of the word "drug" in Cal. Veh. Code § 23102 was unconstitutionally vague. Defendant also contended that the real constitutional problem was not whether insulin was a drug but whether drugs such as insulin when taken normally may have been the basis of a prosecution under § 23102. The court held that no penalty was placed on the use of the drug itself. The penalty was placed upon driving under the influence. No matter how innocently a person may get "under the influence" of intoxicating liquor or drugs or both, his safety and the safety of others required that he not drive. The court held the trial court modified a jury instruction requested by defendant regarding the jury's need to determine whether or not insulin was a drug within § 23102a. The court held although the instruction was confusing and erroneous it was helpful to defendant, and the issue of whether insulin was a drug was not an issue for the jury. The court concluded defendant was not prejudiced.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and the order.

 

1967 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris


Crim. A. No. 4321


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


182 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 837; 5 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 2186



June 10, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Pasadena Judicial District. William M. Martin, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Prosecution for gambling and for knowingly being present at a place where gambling was being conducted.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgment of the Municipal Court for the Pasadena Judicial District (California), which convicted defendants for gambling in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 330 and for knowingly being present at a place where gambling was being conducted, in a violation of a city ordinance. The trial court rejected defendants' offer of proof that the statute and ordinance were deliberately applied differently by reason of race and color.


OVERVIEW: The court found that question before was whether the offered proof was admissible as tending to show deliberate and intentional racial discriminatory enforcement of the criminal laws in violation of the equal protection guarantee of the 14th Amendment to the Constitution of the United States and of Cal. Const. art. I, § 11. The court reversed defendants' convictions and held that the trial court should have permitted defendants to introduce any evidence they offered which tended to show actual intentional discrimination in the enforcement of the statute and ordinance. The court noted that it was not holding that the offered evidence proved the defense of discriminatory enforcement, rather, the court was merely holding that it was admissible in defendants' efforts to prove that defense. The court held that it was error and wholly unsatisfactory to determine whether that defense was sufficient merely on an offer of proof.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case to the trial court with direction ordering that the trial court was to admit all competent evidence not inconsistent with the court's opinion on the issue of the unconstitutional application of the statute and ordinance in question.

 

1968 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Reliable Steel Supply Co. v. Croom


Civ. A. No. 9983


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


181 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 831; 5 Cal. Rptr. 310; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 2065



May 17, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Long Beach Judicial District. Lyman B. Sutter, Judge.

Action to foreclose a mechanic's lien.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of dismissal following sustaining of demurrer to complaint without leave to amend, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant materialman sought review of a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Long Beach Judicial District (California) that dismissed the materialman's action to foreclose a mechanic's lien. The trial court sustained a demurrer filed by the owner and contractor for on the ground that the complaint failed to comply with the notice requirements of Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1193.


OVERVIEW: The materialman recorded a claim of lien for materials furnished and then brought this foreclosure action. The demurrer to the complaint was sustained without leave to amend because the complaint did not allege that the materialman gave written notice to the owner and contractor prior to the filing of the claim of lien as prescribed by § 1193. The materialman argued on appeal that the notice requirement of § 1193 was unconstitutional because it discriminated between materialmen, who were required to provide notice, and certain classes of laborers who were not. The court ruled that pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XX, § 15, both materialmen and laborers had a constitutional right to a lien. The effect of the constitutional provision was to place mechanics, materialmen, artisans, and laborers in the same class. Their equality was thus established by the state Constitution and could not be impaired or destroyed by the legislature. The distinction drawn by the notice requirement of Cal Code Civ. Proc. § 1193 therefore violated the Constitution and the demurrer to the materialman's complaint should not have been sustained.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the materialman's complaint and directed that the owner and contractor should be given reasonable time to answer.

People v. Clinesmith

Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles

CASE SUMMARY:

A subpoena, calling for the attendance of the chief of police, was properly quashed because no affidavit was filed under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1985. Also, if the denial of a continuance was erroneous, it was not prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1970 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Winters


Crim. A. No. 4066


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


171 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 876; 342 P.2d 538; 1959 Cal. App. LEXIS 1913



June 30, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District dismissing complaint without trial. David W. Williams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which dismissed the case against defendant without a trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating state and local gambling laws. The trial court dismissed the case, finding that the state conducted discriminatory enforcement of the gambling laws. On appeal, the court reversed. The court found that the trial court's minute order failed to show the reasons why the dismissal was in the interest of justice as required by Cal. Penal Code § 1385. The dismissal was void because the trial judge acted in a matter where he openly declared his bias and prejudgment without disqualifying himself. The trial judge revealed that he personally had charged that there was discriminatory law enforcement by the chief of police in an exchange of correspondence before the case was called for trial; that he rejected the official statement in response to his charge; and that he acted upon his own extrajudicial belief that there was discriminatory enforcement of the gambling laws against blacks, without receipt of evidence or a hearing. The trial judge exceeded his discretion confided to him under § 1385. The court found that defendant was entitled to present proof, if any, at trial that there had been intentional discrimination based on any improper consideration.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that dismissed the state's case against defendant for violating gambling laws. The court ordered the trial to proceed against defendant. The trial judge could be disqualified at the instance of the state or defendant.

 

1971 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dickenson


Crim. A. No. 770


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


171 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 872; 343 P.2d 809; 1959 Cal. App. LEXIS 1912



June 19, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District. Luther N. Hussey, Judge.

Prosecution for violation of municipal ordinances regulating the automobile wrecking business.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgment of conviction affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions by the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District (California) for violation San Diego, Cal., Municipal Code, Ordinance No. 33.0903.1 and 33.0903.7, which regulated the automobile wrecking business. An application for a writ of prohibition on the ground that the ordinances were unconstitutional was previously denied so that the question could be determined on a trial of the case.


OVERVIEW: San Diego, Cal., Municipal Code, Ordinance No. 33.0903.1 provided that an automobile wrecking establishment must to be surrounded by a solid fence or wall at least six feet in height. Defendant conceded that it was within the power and discretion of the municipality to require a fence but contended that the requirement that the fence must be solid was for no other purpose than the aesthetic one of screening an unsightly business from the view of the passing public. The court determined that because the sole purpose of a solid fence was aesthetic, the requirement was an unreasonable interference with the right to carry on a lawful and legitimate business. San Diego, Cal., Municipal Code, Ordinance No. 33.0903.7 provided that the premises of an automobile wrecking establishment should be so arranged that reasonable inspection or access to all parts of the premises could be had at any time by proper fire, health, police and building authorities. The court found that this ordinance had nothing to do with any aesthetic matter and was neither vague nor uncertain.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for failing to surround his establishment with a solid fence but affirmed his conviction for failing to arrange his premises so that reasonable inspections could be made by authorities.

 

1972 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Reed v. Hollywood Professional School


Civ. A. No. 9703


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


169 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 887; 338 P.2d 633; 1959 Cal. App. LEXIS 2158



April 13, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District. Vernon W. Hunt, Judge.

Action for damages arising from refusal of a private school to enroll a Negro.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of nonsuit affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Black student appealed a judgment from the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which nonsuited her claim for damages under Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51-54. The student alleged that she was refused enrollment in defendant private school based upon her race.


OVERVIEW: The student through her guardian ad litem filed suit against the private school because after solicitous literature was sent to her parents, she was denied enrollment solely because the private school owner stated he could not admit Blackes. The student asserted that the Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51-54 was applicable to the private school because it fell within the meaning of the statute that all other places of public accommodation or amusement, and that the private school's refusal to enroll her created liability under the statute. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of nonsuit because the court held that a private school was not a place of public accommodation or amusement within the meaning of the statute, and admission decisions were left to each institution's discretion because private educational institutions were not public in nature. The court held that the constitutional guarantees applied to state action rather than privation action, and that courts had refused to coerce private institutions in the exercise of lawful discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of nonsuit.

 

1973 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Daniel


Crim. A. No. 65379


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Stanislaus


168 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 788; 337 P.2d 247; 1959 Cal. App. LEXIS 2532



February 16, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court for the Modesto Judicial District dismissing a prosecution for vagrancy. Clarence M. Hanson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed an order from the Municipal Court for the Modesto Judicial District (California), which dismissed a prosecution for vagrancy against defendant, who was charged with being a common drunkard in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(11).


OVERVIEW: The people prosecuted defendant for vagrancy after he was charged with being a common drunkard. When the trial court dismissed the prosecution, the people appealed. The court reversed and set aside the dismissal and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to vacate its order and to restore the case to the calendar. The words common drunkard were sufficiently certain and definite to meet the constitutional requirements. The law was not void for uncertainty or ambiguity. The trial court committed error in dismissing the complaint and charge. The law sufficiently advised defendant, the trial court, and the counsel of the nature of the charge. The court did not see how anyone could fail to understand the meaning of the words common drunkard. Their common use and the availability of the dictionary definitions furnished everybody with the means of knowing exactly what they meant. They were sufficient to warn everyone of the danger of getting persistently and habitually drunk.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and set aside the order, which dismissed the prosecution for vagrancy against defendant, and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to vacate its order and to restore the case to the calendar for further proceedings.

 

1974 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Van Wong


Crim. A. No. 3880


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


165 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 821; 332 P.2d 872; 1958 Cal. App. LEXIS 1361



December 1, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from orders of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District dismissing complaint on sustaining demurrer thereto. Evelle J. Younger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State sought review of the decision of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which dismissed the State's complaint and sustained the demurrer filed by defendant in an action for violation of an ordinance prohibiting sale of event tickets within a certain distance of a stadium.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for violation of a municipal ordinance that prohibited sale of tickets to events within a certain distance of the event forum. The municipal court sustained defendant's demurrer to his prosecution by the State. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the ordinance was patently discriminatory in holding that sales could be made at a ticket booth on the public grounds, but could not be made in a single instance from a citizen's pocket. The State's argument that the ordinance could be enforced at the discretion of the municipality was of no comfort to the court, which held that this was the type of arbitrary and discriminatory aspect that invalidated an ordinance. The ordinance's restrictions did not have a reasonable and nondiscriminatory relationship to the public's right of use of streets and public grounds and the classifications were not based on any inherent difference thereto.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the municipal court's dismissal of the State's action against defendant.

 

1975 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Talley


Crim. A. No. 3865


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


172 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 797; 332 P.2d 447; 1958 Cal. App. LEXIS 1252



November 17, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District and from an order denying a new trial. John G. Barnes, Judge.

Prosecution for distributing handbills in violation of a municipal ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which convicted him of distributing handbills that did not then and there have upon them the name and address of the person who printed, wrote, compiled, or manufactured it, or who caused it to be distributed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the ordinance violated the First and Fourteenth Amendments. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that the First Amendment historically meant "freedom from censorship" or "prior restraints" which prevented free discussion of political questions or governmental action. Free speech had never meant that a speaker was free from accountability for what was written or printed. The requirement that the desired information be placed upon a handbill did not restrict what could be said, except the purely speculative personal possibility that someone might hesitate to identify himself with his own statements therein contained. Accordingly, the court held that the requirement was reasonably germane to the exercise of the police power because it provided a means of determining and securing responsibility for what was said. The state could by general and nondiscriminatory legislation regulate the times, places, and manner of speech, including the distribution of written speech. The court concluded that the ordinance's effect upon freedom of speech was too indirect and insubstantial for it to be unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for distributing a handbill in violation of a municipal ordinance.

 

1976 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Power


Crim. A. No. 221586


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


159 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 869; 324 P.2d 113; 1958 Cal. App. LEXIS 2081; 34 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P71,516



April 4, 1958 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District. Lowell Howe, Judge.

Prosecution for violation of Lab. Code, §§ 204, 216, 222, relating to payment of wages.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction upon three counts of violation of the California Labor Code by the Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District (California). Defendant utilized an employee and paid him the agreed upon wages. It was contended by the state that there was an agreement between defendant and a local and that the employee should have been paid the wages under a union contract.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that he hired the employee at an agreed rate of wages and the employee was paid at that wage and the dispute did not arise until after the termination of employment. The court found that defendant was charged with violation of Cal. Lab. Code § 216, which made it a misdemeanor for one having the ability to pay, to unlawfully refuse to pay wages when due, or who falsely denied the wages with the intent to harass, oppress, hinder, delay, or defraud the employee. The court also found that Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 prohibited imprisonment for civil debts. The court reversed, finding that § 216 did not apply to defendant's situation as the employee was paid the agreed wages and that the employee was not entitled to have enforced the union contract when he agreed to wages on his own. The court also held that Cal. Lab. Code § 204, which made it a misdemeanor not pay wages twice a month and Cal. Lab. Code § 222, which made it a misdemeanor to willfully, and with intent to defraud, withhold any wage arrived at through collective bargaining, were not applicable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded defendant's conviction of three counts of the California Labor Code.

 

1977 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Neal C. Oester, Inc.


Crim. A. No. 34


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Orange


154 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 888; 316 P.2d 784; 1957 Cal. App. LEXIS 1720



October 22, 1957 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court for the Anaheim-Fullerton Judicial District dismissing a criminal complaint. Walter B. Chaffee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of California appealed the judgment of the Municipal Court for the Anaheim-Fullerton Judicial District (California), which dismissed its criminal complaint charging defendants, as an employing unit, with a violation of Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 2110.


OVERVIEW: A criminal complaint was filed against defendants for a violation of § 2110. On a motion by defendants, the trial court dismissed the complaint on the ground that § 2110 was unconstitutional as constituting imprisonment for debt. The State appealed and the court held that the constitutional prohibition against imprisonment for debt applied in criminal cases. The court found that the workers' contributions required under § 2110 were taxes, and that the prohibition against imprisonment for debt applied to taxes. The court also held that § 2110 came within the fraud exception to the constitutional prohibition against imprisonment for debt because an employer who knowingly withheld the deductions and then willfully failed to pay them did an act against good morals and fair dealing and necessarily intentionally did an act which prejudiced the rights of the employee. The court found that such conduct amounted to "a case of fraud." The court further held that the word "willfully" in § 2110 qualified not only the word "fails," but also "is financially unable" and that a showing of willfulness was necessary for a conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment dismissing the State's complaint and remanded the case with instructions to set aside the order of dismissal and proceed with the trial.

Prosecution for payment of wages in illegal form in violation of Lab. Code, § 212.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which entered his guilty plea and sentenced him to 12 jail terms for 12 violations of Cal. Lab. Code § 212 for payment of wages in an illegal form.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating § 212 by writing checks for payment of wages when he had insufficient funds to pay them. He pleaded guilty and was sentenced. The court did note that a more careful pleading which pointed to the particular failings in the instruments would be fairer to defendant but that defendant had not demurred to the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that entered defendant's guilty plea and sentenced him to 12 terms in prison.

 

1979 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Binzley


Crim. A. No. 3505


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


146 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 889; 303 P.2d 903; 1956 Cal. App. LEXIS 1554



November 19, 1956 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Glendale Judicial District. Kenneth A. White, Judge.

Prosecution for unlawfully maintaining a bus storage facility contrary to a municipal code section.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of dismissal on sustaining defendant's demurrer to complaint and the People's statement that they did not intend to amend, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prosecution charging defendant with unlawfully maintaining a bus storage facility contrary to Glendale, Cal., Mun. Code § 307, plaintiff, the People of the State of California, appealed from the Municipal Court for the Glendale Judicial District (California), which sustained defendant's demurrer to the complaint and discharged defendant.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court stated that the M2 zone designated in § 307 was a zone where any use "not prohibited in the city" was expressly permitted. Nowhere in § 307 was a "bus storage facility" expressly permitted, with the result that it was not expressly allowed in any of the zones other than zone M2, but neither was it expressly prohibited in the city, and so could be maintained in that part of the city falling within zone M2. The court agreed with the trial court that one of the items listed in § 307 was too vague and uncertain to be valid legislation. However, it did not follow that a use, not one of the 22 expressly permitted, was, because of the uncertainty of item 23, thereby authorized. The court stated that the complaint should have alleged that defendant maintained his bus storage facility in a C3 zone, but this defect was not particularized in the demurrer, and in view of the terms of § 307, no misunderstanding was possible as to the charge made.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of dismissal with directions to overrule defendant's demurrer and to arraign him for a plea.
