1981 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Benane v. International Harvester Co.


Civ. A. No. 178


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Alameda


142 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 874; 299 P.2d 750; 1956 Cal. App. LEXIS 2065; 30 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P70,078



June 28, 1956 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Oakland-Piedmont Judicial District. Joseph A. Murphy, Judge.

Action for wages deducted during absence from employment to vote at a general election.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for defendant reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought review of the decision of the Municipal Court for the Oakland-Piedmont Judicial District (California), which rendered judgment in favor of appellee employer in the employees' action to recover wages that were deducted from the paycheck of each because of a two-hour absence during regular working hours on a general election day due to voting.


OVERVIEW:  Cal. Elec. Code § 5699 provided that every voter shall have been entitled to two consecutive hours during his employment in which to vote and was not liable for any penalty nor shall any deduction have been made on account of the absence from the usual salary or wages. The issues on appeal were whether § 5699 was constitutional, was § 5699 ambiguous and therefore subject to judicial interpretation, and what was the effect of the collective bargaining agreement that provided that the employees were allowed time off, without pay, in order to vote. The court reversed the judgment and held that the waiver provision in the collective bargaining agreement was invalid not only because it was against public policy, but also because it was contrary to an express provision of the law. The court determined that the language of § 5699 was clear and unambiguous and therefore there was no room for statutory construction. The legislature intended the statute to have been a safeguard to the right of suffrage. Because there was no waiver of their right to compensation while taking time off to vote, the employees were entitled to their compensation for the two-hour time period.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the employer in the employees' action for compensation and directed the trial court to enter judgment for the employees as requested.

 

1982 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Alberts


Crim. A. No. 3350


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


138 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 909; 292 P.2d 90; 1955 Cal. App. LEXIS 1319



December 29, 1955 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied 138 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 909 at 911.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for Beverly Hills Judicial District and from an order denying a new trial. Charles J. Griffin, Judge.

Prosecution for keeping obscene and indecent books for sale and for having lewdly written, composed and published an advertisement of them.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of conviction for the crime of keeping obscene and indecent books for sale and for having lewdly written, composed, and published an advertisement of them, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 311, that was entered by the Municipal Court for Beverly Hills Judicial District (California). Defendant also sought review of the trial court's order denying defendant a new trial.


OVERVIEW: The complaint charged all of the offenses identified in § 311(3), even though there was no evidence that defendant had committed the majority of the offenses set forth in § 311(3). The court ruled, however, that there was no legal error to charge all of the offenses within one count. The court also observed that even if an error had been made, defendant failed to make such an objection by demurrer. Moreover, the court noted that defendant was not prejudiced by the form of the complaint because defendant would have been no better off if the complaint had merely charged that he had kept obscene books for sale. The court also ruled that the words "obscene and indecent," as they were used in § 311, were not unconstitutionally indefinite. Prior decisions had established that the meaning of these words was well understood. Finally, the court rejected the argument that defendant's use of the United States mail to distribute the lewd books did not render the statute inoperative.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and order denying a new trial.

 

1983 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Plywood Mfrs. of California


Crim. A. Nos. 3284, 3286, 3303-3306, 3327


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


137 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 859; 291 P.2d 587; 1955 Cal. App. LEXIS 1274



November 21, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS (Crim. A. 3284) from judgments of the Municipal Court for the San Antonio Judicial District and from orders denying motions in arrest of judgment. Harry R. Simon, Judge.

APPEAL (Crim. A. 3286) from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Compton Judicial District and from an order denying a new trial. Donald E. Dunbar, Judge.

APPEALS (Crim. A. 3303-3306) from judgments of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District and from orders denying a new trial. Marcus R. Brandler, Judge.

APPEAL (Crim. A. 3327) from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District and from an order denying a new trial. Otto B. Willett, Judge.

Prosecutions for emitting air contaminants.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed; appeals from orders dismissed. Reversed. Affirmed. Affirmed. One judgment of conviction reversed; others affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the decisions of four California municipal courts, which convicted defendants of smog violations. The numerous actions were consolidated on appeal.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected the contention of defendants that the terms employed in Cal. Health & Safety Code § 24242 rendered the section unconstitutionally indefinite. The court concluded that both subsections of § 24242 were, on their face, sufficiently definite to constitute criminal statutes; that to warrant a conviction under the section proof must have been made that it was a contaminant that was discharged into the atmosphere, and that the contaminant was either of a shade as dark or darker than Ringelmann No. 2, or that it obstructed the vision at least as much as would be smoke that was as dark as Ringelmann No. 2. The court further rejected the contention that § 24242 was unconstitutional because the ordinary person, having no special training, would not have been able to tell whether his smoke was as dark as Ringelmann No. 2. The court reversed the order denying one defendant a new trial, holding that the error in failing to advise the jury that each essential fact had to be proven beyond a reasonable doubt could not have been said to have been without prejudice. The court affirmed the remaining judgments, holding that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the convictions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying one defendant a new trial from his convictions for smog violations. The court affirmed the remaining convictions of the remaining defendants for smog violations.

 

1984 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Villarino


Crim. A. No. 196365


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


134 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 893; 286 P.2d 86; 1955 Cal. App. LEXIS 1851



June 30, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District. James C. Toothaker, Judge.

Prosecution for violation of city ordinance prohibiting use of or addiction to narcotics.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with the offense of being "unlawfully addicted to the use of narcotics and used narcotics" in violation of the City of San Diego Ordinance, § 52.65. The Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District (California) dismissed the case on defendant's motion that asserted § 52.65 conflicted with Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721 and was therefore unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 11. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: When the state elected to proceed under the city ordinance rather than state law, defendant raised the question of the city ordinance's validity. The court held that the City of San Diego Ordinance, § 52.65 was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. XI, §11 because a state statute, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721, outlawed the very same activity. The court held that the local ordinance covered the same ground as the penal code and was therefore invalid because it conflicted with the general laws of the state. The court reasoned that if defendant were first tried and convicted or acquitted under the local ordinance, he could not then be tried for the same offense under the state statute. In affirming the dismissal, the court held that its decision did not preclude the state from filing a new complaint under § 11721.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that dismissed defendant's charges of using or being addicted to narcotics in violation of a local ordinance.

 

1985 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


Crim. A. No. 3269


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


133 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 777; 284 P.2d 203; 1955 Cal. App. LEXIS 1702; 36 L.R.R.M. 2249; 28 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P69,265



May 18, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court for the San Jose Judicial District dismissing an action. Roland J. Brownsberger, Judge.

Prosecution for advertising in violation of Lab. Code, § 973.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Appeal from order dismissing action reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of California appealed the order of the Municipal Court for the San Jose Judicial District (California), which dismissed the prosecution against defendant for unlawful advertising in violation of Cal. Lab. Code § 973.


OVERVIEW: While a strike was in active progress, defendant placed an advertisement in the local newspaper soliciting persons to work for him without stating that a strike or other trade dispute was going on. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of § 973, and the trial court dismissed the prosecution. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the trial court exceeded its authority in dismissing the action based on its finding that the facts stated did not constitute a public offense; the trial court was only authorized by statute to dismiss the prosecution in the furtherance of justice. Further, the trial court was in error in holding that § 973 was invalid. The statute's intent was to protect workers from misrepresentation, and the terms of the statute were definite and understandable. Thus, § 973 was not unconstitutional for being vague and uncertain, and a new trial was warranted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and remanded with directions.

 

1986 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wright


Civ. A. Nos. 191979, 191980


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


131 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 853; 281 P.2d 384; 1955 Cal. App. LEXIS 2138



February 15, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from judgments of the Municipal Court for Oceanside Judicial District. L. W. Cottingham, Judge.

Prosecutions for violations of Civil Engineers' Act and Architects' Act.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of dismissal in Civ. No. 191979, reversed; judgment of dismissal in Civ. No. 191980, affirmed. Judgment of dismissal in prosecution for violation of Civil Engineers' Act, affirmed; judgment of dismissal in other prosecution, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of judgments of the Municipal Court for Oceanside Judicial District (California), which dismissed two separate actions filed against respondent for violation of the Civil Engineers' Act and the Architect's Act.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged in two separate actions with the violation of the Civil Engineers' Act and the Architects' Act. Respondent was not certified or licensed under either act. The trial court sustained a demurrer to each amended complaint without leave to amend. The state contended that in each case the amended complaint stated a public offense. The court found that the Civil Engineers' Act, insofar as a nonregistered practitioner was forbidden to advertise, was clear and justified as a regulatory measure. The court held that because those allegations charged respondent with a crime defined in the act, they stated a public offense. The court found that the amended complaint regarding the Architects' Act failed to state a public offense in the terms of the statute because respondent's activities with respect to a concrete building over one story in height were not ad hoc prohibited, and upon those considerations, the complaint in the engineer's case failed to state a public offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of dismissal in prosecution of the Civil Engineers' Act and affirmed the judgment of dismissal in the prosecution of the Architects' Act.

 

1987 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Housing Authority of Los Angeles v. Cordova


Civ. A. No. 8550


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


130 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 883; 279 P.2d 215; 1955 Cal. App. LEXIS 1991



January 19, 1955 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District. Lucius P. Green, Judge.

Action in unlawful detainer.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Summary judgment for plaintiff reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant tenants appealed the judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which granted plaintiff housing authority's motion for summary judgment in its action for unlawful detainer of housing which the authority manages as landlord.


OVERVIEW: The tenants refused to sign a certificate provided by the housing authority, by which the tenants would have disavowed membership in certain organizations listed as subversive. When the tenants refused to sign the certificate, the housing authority elected to terminate their leases under a clause permitting termination upon 10 days' notice. When the tenants refused to leave, the authority brought an action in unlawful detainer. The trial court granted the housing authority's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court noted that membership in the organizations designated by the attorney general as subversive did not, even for the purposes of public employment, determine that the employees were in fact disloyal to the United States. The court held that, once the government elected to provide low-income housing, it could not discriminate among its citizens in granting access to that housing. The court further held that the purpose for the act which created the housing authority was to eradicate slums, not to combat subversive organizations. The goal of combating subversives was not advanced by making such people live in slums.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case for a new trial of the issues outlined in the court's decision.

 

1988 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Arnold


Crim. A. No. 3141


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


127 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 844; 273 P.2d 711; 1954 Cal. App. LEXIS 1419



August 26, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District. Ben Koenig, Judge.

Prosecution for violation of municipal code section prohibiting distribution of any handbill which does not indicate thereon its authorship and identity of person or persons causing its distribution.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his conviction in the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California) for a violation of Los Angeles, Cal. Mun. Code § 28.06, which prohibited the distribution of any handbill that did not indicate thereon its authorship and the identity of the person or persons causing its distribution. Defendant contended that the ordinance violated his constitutional guarantee of freedom of speech and of the press.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for passing handbills that did not contain the required information of authorship. He contended that the freedoms of speech and of the press necessarily included the right of a writer to remain anonymous. The court disagreed. Cal. Const. art. I, § 9 specifically provided, along with a guaranty of free speech, that citizens were responsible for an abuse of that right. The court held that the responsibility for one's speech was also necessarily implied in the First Amendment guaranties of freedom of speech and of the press. There was no constitutional right to remain anonymous. The manner of exercising the right of free speech could be regulated under the state's police power. Here the regulation provided a means by which one who was defamed could seek redress and also served to insure the effective enforcement of laws against obscenity. The court held that the ordinance did not offend either the federal or state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1989 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Agran v. Shapiro


Civ. A. No. 8212


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


127 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 807; 273 P.2d 619; 1954 Cal. App. LEXIS 1413; 46 A.F.T.R. (P-H) 896



June 14, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District. Kenneth L. Holaday, Judge.

Action to recover for professional services.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for plaintiff reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff accountant filed an action to recover a fee owed by defendant clients for services relating to the accountant's work in researching and negotiating a tax deduction on the client's behalf. The Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California) entered judgment for the accountant. The clients appealed.


OVERVIEW: The accountant, an agent admitted to practice before the Treasury Department, had prepared tax returns for the clients for several years. When the Internal Revenue Service denied a carry-back business loss claimed by the clients, the accountant researched the issue, prepared a statement, and actively negotiated the claim. The court held that the accountant had engaged in the unauthorized practice of law. In determining that the accountant's actions were the unauthorized practice of law, the court rejected the "incidental test," which looked to whether the services rendered were incident to accounting work. Instead, the court looked to 31 C.F.R. § 10.2(f), subtit. A, part 10, which granted a nonattorney agent certain privileges before the Treasury Department, but did not authorize persons not members of the bar to practice law. Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6126 imposed criminal penalties for the unauthorized practice of law. Because the contract between the accountant and the clients involved the violation of a penal statute, the trial court had an obligation to dismiss the action so that neither party could take anything from the other.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the accountant and remanded the action for a new trial.

 

1990 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Amdur


Crim. A. No. 28


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Alameda


123 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 951; 267 P.2d 445; 1954 Cal. App. LEXIS 1284



February 23, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Berkeley-Albany Judicial District. Redmond C. Staats, Jr., Judge.

Prosecution for placing obstruction on public sidewalk.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Municipal Court for the Berkeley-Albany Judicial District (California) convicting him of violating Berkeley, Cal., Ordinance 3262-N.S., § 12.1 by placing a table on a public sidewalk which obstructed, restricted, and prevented the use of a portion of the sidewalk.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that Berkeley, Cal., Ordinance 3262-N.S. was unconstitutional as written and as applied. Determining that the trial court had failed to admit all competent evidence on the issue of the unconstitutional application of the ordinance, the court concluded that defendant was entitled to a new trial. The court held that Berkeley, Cal., Ordinance No. 3262-N.S. was constitutional as written because the administration of an ordinance which regulated activities that were mere privileges could be left to the uncontrolled discretion of the licensing authority. Further, even though the city was under no duty to permit the use of tables on its sidewalks, if it had granted that privilege to some, it could not have denied the same privilege to others under similar circumstances merely because its officials disagreed with their views.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and ordered a new trial.

 

1991 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Turner


Crim A. No. 3026


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


121 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 861; 263 P.2d 685; 1953 Cal. App. LEXIS 1439



November 24, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of Los Angeles Judicial District, from order denying a new trial, and from other orders. Roger Alton Pfaff, Judge.

Prosecution of parents for having neglected and refused to send children to a public school in violation of Ed. Code, § 16601.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order denying a new trial affirmed; appeals from other orders dismissed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged a judgment of the Municipal Court of Los Angeles Judicial District (California) denying a new trial after the parents were convicted of having neglected and refused to send their children to a public school in violation of Cal. Educ. Code § 16601.


OVERVIEW: The parents were convicted of violating Cal. Educ. Code § 16601 after they refused to send their three children to a public school. On appeal of the denial of the parents' motion for a new trial, they argued, inter alia, that § 16601 was unconstitutional in that it deprives parents of the right to determine how and where their children may be educated. The court agreed that if the statute, without qualification or exception, required parents to place their children in public schools, it would indeed be unconstitutional. However, the court determined that the statute recognizes the right of parents not to place their children in public schools if they elect to have them educated in a private school or through the medium of a private tutor or other person possessing certain specified qualifications. The parents' claim that they came within the classification of a "private school" within the meaning of Cal. Educ. Code § 16624 was without merit. Thus, the order denying the parents' motion for a new trial was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and order denying the parents' motion for a new trial. The appeals from the other orders were dismissed.

 

1992 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sevel


Crim. A. No. 181160


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


120 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 907; 261 P.2d 359; 1953 Cal. App. LEXIS 2041



September 3, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court of the South Bay Judicial District dismissing a complaint. Lowell Howe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with unlawfully operating an automobile junkyard without a fence enclosure, a violation of San Diego County, Cal., Ordinance No. 953 (amended 1950). Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the complaint, claiming that the ordinance was in derogation of his rights under the Due Process Clause. The Municipal Court of the South Bay Judicial District (California) granted defendant's motion. The People appealed the order.


OVERVIEW: The trial court did not provide a reason for the dismissal. On appeal, the People claimed that the ordinance was valid. The court reviewed the ordinance and noted that § 7 permitted the severability of its provisions. The court directed its attention to the constitutionality of § 1(a) of the ordinance, which related to the requirement that the junkyard business premises be enclosed by a fence. The court noted that a junkyard business was subject to police regulation to prevent unscrupulous persons from disposing of stolen property through such medium. Other provisions of the ordinance described the fire risks involved from gaseous fumes of unused gasoline in tanks and greasy upholstery of secondhand automobiles, which reasonably suggested police regulation by adequate housing or fence or wall enclosure. The court found nothing in § 1(a) violated defendant's constitutional right to conduct his lawful business. The regulation was a reasonable restraint in the interest of the public safety and general welfare.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that dismissed the complaint.

 

1993 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Finch


Crim. A. No. 3008


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


119 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 892; 258 P.2d 1124; 1953 Cal. App. LEXIS 1300



June 23, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court of the El Monte Judicial District dismissing an action. John K. Otis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People sought review of a decision of the Municipal Court of the El Monte Judicial District, (California), which dismissed the charge against defendant for violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 673.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had allegedly modified the exhaust system of his car in a manner that increased the noise of the motor over that of the original muffler. After a jury had been selected and one witness was sworn, defendant moved for a dismissal on the ground that § 673 was unconstitutional, which the trial court granted. The court held that Cal. Pen. Code § 1466 authorized the State to appeal from an order or judgment dismissing or otherwise terminating an action before a defendant had been placed in jeopardy or where the defendant had waived jeopardy. Further, since the jury was discharged with the consent of defendant without rendering a verdict, there was no jeopardy and no acquittal. Hence, jeopardy did not attach at defendant's trial. Moreover, Cal. Pen. Code § 673 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing the action, and the case was remanded to the trial court for trial.

 

1994 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. King


Civ. A. No. 175244


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


115 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 875; 252 P.2d 78; 1952 Cal. App. LEXIS 1789



December 12, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court of San Diego Judicial District sustaining demurrer, without leave to amend, to complaint charging violation of a city ordinance. A. F. Molina, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People challenged a judgment of the Municipal Court of San Diego Judicial District (California), which sustained defendant's demurrer, without leave to amend, to the People's complaint alleging that defendant violated San Diego, Cal., Mun. Code, Ordinance No. 33.1541.1 by unlawfully mingling with a patron of the on-sale liquor establishment where defendant worked.


OVERVIEW: The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer based on a finding that the word "mingle" as used in No. 33.1541.1 was vague, indefinite, and uncertain, thereby rendering the ordinance unconstitutional. On appeal, the court disagreed and reversed. A legislative enactment was inoperative if a definite meaning could not be given to its terms in order to ensure the giving of fair warning in ordinary language as to what conduct would be criminal under given circumstances. Some definite meaning was to given to the words used in order to give them effect, and the legislative intent was to be gathered from the whole act. After construing the word "mingle" in context with other words of the ordinance, the court found that the ordinance clearly sought to prohibit activities of female employees in liquor establishments other than those normally, usually, and properly associated with that occupation and that the language used was adequate, unambiguous, and understandable. Finally, the court held that the extent of the alleged impropriety depended on the facts in each case, such that the question of whether defendant's actions were improper was one for the trier of the fact to determine.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the matter with directions to overrule the demurrer and to proceed with defendant's arraignment and trial.

 

1995 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kiser


Crim. A. No. 2870


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


112 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 903; 245 P.2d 1125; 1952 Cal. App. LEXIS 1122



July 2, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of the City of Los Angeles. Lewis Drucker, Judge.

Prosecution for violating Veh. Code, § 678.5, relating to mudguards.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his conviction in the Municipal Court of the City of Los Angeles (California) for operating a motor vehicle without mudguards in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 678.5. He contended that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and that he did not violate the statute.


OVERVIEW: Section 678.5 required that vehicles be equipped with adequate protection to effectively minimize the spray or splash of water or mud to the rear of the vehicle. Defendant contended that the terms "adequate," "effectively," and "minimize" introduced fatal uncertainty into the statute. The court disagreed. It held that the terms did not create an unascertainable standard of guilt. At the time that he was charged with the offense the weather was dry and there was no mud on the road. Defendant was driving a truck with the mud flaps rolled up. He contended that his vehicle was properly equipped and that if it started raining he could comply with the statute by merely rolling his mud flaps down. The court held that the statute required that the mudguards be in place at all times, or if they were not, that there be some kind of mechanism within the cab of the truck by which defendant could lower them. It did not contemplate that defendant would have to stop the truck and get out to roll them down.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1996 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Saad


Crim. A. No. 2768


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


105 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 851; 234 P.2d 785; 1951 Cal. App. LEXIS 1559



August 8, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from a judgment of the Arcadia City Court, Monrovia Township, County of Los Angeles, and from a verdict and order denying a new trial. David T. Sweet, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from judgment reversed with directions; appeals from verdict and order dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Arcadia City Court, Monrovia Township, County of Los Angeles (California), convicting him refusing to leave a race track after he had previously been ejected from other race tracks for practices inimical to the public interest, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 337.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with refusing to leave a race track when requested due to prior ejections from race tracks based on practices inimical to the public interest. Section 337.5 established a misdemeanor if a person refused a request to leave a race track if the person had been ejected from any race track for touting or practices inimical to the public interest. The court on appeal reversed the judgment of conviction, holding that § 337.5 was void as a violation of the due process requirements of the Fourteenth Amendment. Due process required that a penal statute had to provide notice of the conduct criminalized by the statute. The court concluded that the element of the crime identified by the phrase "practices inimical to the public interest" was not an ascertainable standard of guilt. The statute was therefore unconstitutionally vague and indefinite and could not be a viable basis for a conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of conviction with directions to the trial court to dismiss the action.

 

1997 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. McCormick


Crim. A. Nos. 2686, 2695


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


102 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 954; 228 P.2d 349; 1951 Cal. App. LEXIS 1414



February 27, 1951 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 27, 1951.


PRIOR HISTORY: In Crim. A. No. 2686: APPEAL from a judgment of the Justice's Court of San Antonio Township. Stanley Moffatt, Judge.

Prosecutions for failure to register as members of a communist organization, as required by county ordinance.

In Crim. A. No. 2695: APPEAL from a judgment of the Justice's Court of Belvedere Township. Myer B. Marion, Judge.

Prosecutions for failure to register as members of a communist organization, as required by county ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of dismissal after sustaining demurrers to complaints, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People appealed from a judgment from the Justice's Court of San Antonio Township (California) and from the Justice's Court of Belvedere Township (California) sustaining defendants' demurrer to complaints filed against each defendant for failing to register as members of a communist organization, as required by Los Angeles County, Cal. Ordinance 5578.


OVERVIEW: Defendants claimed that the ordinance was without void due to constitutional restrictions. The court agreed, concluding that the ordinance violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 13 as compliance with the ordinance amounted to a virtual confession that the registrant violated the Criminal Syndicalism Act, 1919 Stats. p. 281; Gen. Laws tit. 8428 (Deering). As the danger of self-incrimination appeared on the mere reading of the ordinance, the constitutionality of the ordinance was fairly at issue under the attack of a demurrer. The ordinance was not a simple police power regulation requiring the identification of citizens as it compelled a citizen violating the Act to register and the registration provided the clues to establish his guilt. The fact that the registration statements were unavailable for public examination granted no immunity from prosecution. Moreover, the evidence of a registration was admissible despite its compulsory element. The court rejected the Peoples' contention that the ordinance did not violate the rule against self-incrimination on the basis that it required a person to state a fact which might incriminate him under an offense created by another government entity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments appealed from by the People.

 

1998 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. International Steel Corp.


Crim. A. No. 2654


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, Los Angeles


102 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 935; 226 P.2d 587; 1951 Cal. App. LEXIS 1411



January 25, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from judgments of the Justice's Court of Compton Township. Ralph C. Dills, Judge

Prosecution for air pollution.



DISPOSITION: Some judgments affirmed; other judgments reversed. Judgments against defendants International Steel Corporation and Hochman, affirmed; judgments against defendant Olmstead, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, corporation, manager, and secretary, sought review of the judgments of the Justice's Court of Compton Township (California) of conviction for violations of Cal. Health and Safety Code § 24242, arising from the discharge of smoke from a place of business.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted on charges of violating Cal. Health and Safety Code § 24242. One defendant was a corporation, while the other defendants were the manager and the secretary. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgments of conviction against the corporation and the manager. First, the court held that the statutory mode of determining and stating the density and opacity of smoke by referring to an official publication of the United States Bureau of Mines was a reasonably certain mode, and the statute was not lacking in certainty. Next, the court held that the application of the statute to industrial areas, while exempting agricultural operations, was not an unreasonable classification by the legislature. However, as to the secretary of the corporation, the court held that he was merely a ministerial officer, without authority to transact the business of the corporation upon his independent judgment. Thus, he was not criminally answerable for the act of the corporation, if he was not personally a participant. The judgment against the secretary was reversed. The case was remanded to the municipal court for a new trial as to him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the municipal court's judgment of convictions of two defendants, the corporation and its manager, for violation of air pollution control laws, but reversed the judgment of conviction against defendant, the secretary of the corporation, and remanded the case for a new trial as to him.

 

1999 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ballarini v. Schlage Lock Co.


Civ. A. No. 2074


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Francisco


100 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 859; 226 P.2d 771; 1950 Cal. App. LEXIS 1307



November 17, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Clarence W. Morris, Judge.

Action to recover wages for time taken off to vote, under Elec. Code, § 5699.



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff modified and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employers challenged a decision of the Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found in favor of respondent employees in their action to recover wages for time taken off to vote under Cal. Elec. Code § 5699.


OVERVIEW: The 48 employees took two hours time off on November 8, 1948, a general election day, to vote. They successfully claimed their wages for those hours under Cal. Elec. Code § 5699. The employers alleged that the statute was unconstitutional. The court disagreed, affirming the result below with a small modification for employees who did not establish that they went to vote that day. The court found that the statute was a proper exercise of the state's police power. Its means were reasonably necessary to accomplish its important purpose, and it was not unduly oppressive on the employers. Instead, the employers were presumed to have taken the statute into account when it made contracts with the employees.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reduced to the sum of $ 80, and as so reduced it was affirmed.

 

2000 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Christie


Crim. A. No. 154944


Appellate Department, Superior Court of California, San Diego


95 Cal. App. 2d Supp. 919; 212 P.2d 629; 1949 Cal. App. LEXIS 1136



November 25, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of the City of San Diego. Edwin L. Thacher, Judge pro tem. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Prosecution of a chiropractor for illegal use of word "physician" on his office window and business cards.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a chiropractor, appealed his conviction, by a jury, in the Municipal Court of the City of San Diego (California), for his illegal use of the word "physician" on his office window and business cards.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a licensed chiropractor, held himself out to the public by business cards and a sign on his office window as a "chiropractic physician." He was convicted in a trial by jury of violating § 15 of the Chiropractic Act (Act), which restricted the use of the term "physician" only to practitioners who were authorized to heal with drugs or medicines. The jury had been instructed that if they found that defendant used the word "physician" they had to find him guilty. Defendant appealed, contending that his use of the term "chiropractic physician" was not deceptive of his status as a licensed chiropractor. The court held that defendant, under § 15, was specifically denied the right to employ the word "physician," because he professed to heal without drugs or medicines. The use of the word "physician" by a practitioner of the healing arts implied a license to practice with drugs, which the chiropractor did not have. The distinction made by the Act between practitioners who used drugs and those who did not was a reasonable classification. Therefore, there was no error in the instruction given the jury at the trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.
